### Question 1: CLAIMS-1

**Query:** How do I file a VA disability claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.46s

**Answer:**

To file a VA disability claim, you have several options:

- Use a Veterans Service Officer (VSO) to assist you with the claim. You can find a VSO near you through the VA website¹³.
- File online yourself at VA.gov through the disability claim filing section³.
- Complete and submit VA Form 21-526EZ either online, by fax (844-531-7818), in person at a VA Regional Office, or by mail to the Claims Intake Center in Janesville, WI³.
- If you want to file a Fully Developed Claim (FDC) or a Board of Veterans’ Appeals (BDD) claim, you can also do so online or with the help of a VSO².
- You can start with an Intent to File to preserve your effective date, which can be submitted online, by phone, by mail, fax, or in person using VA Form 21-0966⁶.

Important notes:
- You do not need to have a diagnosis to file a claim².
- If you file a new claim while another is active, the VA will combine the claims but may close the new claim initially³.
- The VA uses the date your claim is processed, not the postmark date, to determine your effective date³⁷.

It is strongly recommended to use a VSO to help you through the process for best results¹⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing a VA Disability Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Filing a BDD Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
³ [Filing a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁴ [How to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
⁶ [Starting an Intent to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
⁷ [Submitting a Supplemental Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 2: CLAIMS-2

**Query:** What forms do I need to file a claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 6.94s

**Answer:**

To file a VA disability claim, the primary form you need is:

- **VA Form 21-526EZ** (Application for Disability Compensation and Related Compensation Benefits)

You can submit this form:

- Online at VA.gov
- By fax to 844-531-7818
- In person at your nearest VA Regional Office
- By mail to:

  ```
  Department of Veterans Affairs
  Claims Intake Center
  PO Box 4444
  Janesville, WI 53547-4444
  ```

If you are filing a claim related to Dependency and Indemnity Compensation or other related benefits, additional forms include:

- VA Form 21P-534EZ (Application for Dependency and Indemnity Compensation, Death Pension and Accrued Benefits)
- VA Form 21P-535 (Application for Dependency and Indemnity Compensation by Parent(s))
- VA Form 21P-601 (Application for Accrued Amounts Due a Deceased Beneficiary)
- VA Form 21P-0847 (Request for Substitution of Claimant Upon Death Claimant)

It is recommended to use a Veterans Service Officer (VSO) to assist with filing claims, but you can file on your own using these forms.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 4: CLAIMS-4

**Query:** What is a C&P exam?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.4s

**Answer:**

A C&P (Compensation and Pension) exam is a medical examination that veterans may be scheduled for after submitting a disability claim to the VA. The purpose of the exam is to evaluate the veteran's medical condition related to their claim. Veterans can request a specific contractor or VA medical center for the exam or request a tele-health appointment if preferred, and the VA will attempt to honor these requests if reasonable and possible. The exam helps the VA determine the severity of the veteran's condition and the appropriate disability rating. Veterans can also review Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs) related to their condition to understand how the exam will be conducted and may have a physician complete a DBQ on their behalf to submit with their claim¹⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [Compensation & Pension Examinations (C&P)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁵ [After Submitting a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Sources Cited:**
- [[Compensation & Pension Examinations (C&P)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)
- [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)
- [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)
- [After Submitting a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 5: CLAIMS-5

**Query:** How do I prepare for a C&P exam?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 8.12s

**Answer:**

To prepare for a Compensation and Pension (C&P) exam, you can take the following steps:

- Review the applicable Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQ) related to your condition. DBQs give you an idea of what the exam will cover and the types of questions or tests you might expect. You can find DBQs for various conditions such as cervical spine, thoracolumbar spine, traumatic brain injury (TBI), cranial nerve conditions, arthritis, fibromyalgia, osteomyelitis, breast conditions, and gynecological conditions³⁴⁵⁶⁷.

- If you want, you may ask your treating physician or another doctor to complete a DBQ on your behalf and submit it with your claim. This can help provide detailed medical information to the VA before your exam³⁴⁵⁶⁷.

- You can include a personal statement with your claim if you want to request a specific contractor or VA medical center for your exam, or if you prefer a tele-health appointment for your mental health exam. The VA will try to honor reasonable requests¹.

- Keep your contact information (mailing address, email, phone number) up to date with the VA to ensure you receive timely notice of your exam appointment⁴.

By reviewing the DBQ relevant to your condition and preparing any personal statements or medical documentation, you can be better prepared for your C&P exam.

**Sources:**

² [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 6: CLAIMS-6

**Query:** What happens after I file a claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 2.54s

**Answer:**

After you file a claim, the next step in the process typically involves the Compensation & Pension Exam (C&P). Veterans can expect to be scheduled for a C&P exam shortly after filing their claim. However, in some cases, a Veteran might not get a C&P exam at all, or it could take several months before one is scheduled. It is important to keep your mailing address, email address, and phone number up-to-date with the VA to avoid missing any notices about the examination¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [After Submitting a Claim - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 7: CLAIMS-7

**Query:** Can I track my claim status?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 1.59s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can track your claim status. Currently, claim trackers will show your claim status as either:

- "We don't know your status"; or
- "A reviewer is examining your new evidence".

These statuses will remain until the claim is actually closed out¹.

### Question 8: CLAIMS-8

**Query:** What is an Intent to File?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.04s

**Answer:**

An Intent to File is the first step in filing a VA disability claim. It is important because it preserves your effective date, which determines when you start receiving payments from the VA. For recently separated Veterans, an Intent to File can be automatically opened the day after separation, and if filed before the one-year separation anniversary, it allows up to an additional year to file the claim while still receiving back pay from the day after separation¹.

You can submit an Intent to File in several ways, including online at VA.gov, through a Veterans Service Officer (VSO), by phone, in person using VA-Form 21-0966, by fax, or by mail. The Intent to File is valid for one year and is good for one use only, although a single claim can cover multiple conditions. If multiple claims or appeals are filed on the same day, they all fall under the same Intent to File².

**Sources:**
¹ [Intent to File - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Starting an Intent to File - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Intent to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Starting an Intent to File](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Confidence:** 0.6868882179260254

### Question 9: CLAIMS-9

**Query:** How do I get my service records?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 8.78s

**Answer:**

To get your service records, including awards, DD-214, enlistment paperwork, orders, and personnel actions, the process depends on your branch of service and when you separated:

- For Air Force, Army, Marine Corps, Navy, Coast Guard, Public Health Services, NOAA, and Space Force, the method varies by separation date.
- If your branch and separation date fall under the category marked with an asterisk (*), you need to submit a request to the National Personnel Records Center (NPRC) at https://vetrecs.archives.gov/s/veteran-request.
- If your branch and separation date fall under the category marked with an at symbol (@), you can request your personnel file through the Defense Personnel Records Information System (DPRIS) by clicking [HERE](https://milconnect.dmdc.osd.mil/milconnect/protected/portlet/dpris) and following the path: Correspondence/Documentation > Defense Personnel Records Information > Request Personnel File¹.

For example:

| Branch of Service | Period of Service Ended | How to Obtain |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Air Force before 10/1/2004 | * | Request from NPRC |
| Air Force after 9/30/2004 | @ | Request via DPRIS |
| Army before 10/1/1994 | * | Request from NPRC |
| Army after 9/30/1994 | @ | Request via DPRIS |
| Marine Corps before 1/1/1999 | * | Request from NPRC |
| Marine Corps after 12/31/1998 | @ | Request via DPRIS |
| Navy before 1/1/1995 | * | Request from NPRC |
| Navy after 12/31/1994 | @ | Request via DPRIS |
| Coast Guard any | * | Request from NPRC |
| Space Force any | @ | Request via DPRIS |

This will get you your military personnel records.

If you want your Service Treatment Records (STRs) or military medical records, the process depends on your branch and separation date as well, with options including:

- Accessing STRs from the MHS GENESIS Patient Portal if your service ended on or after 4/1/2025.
- Sending an SF 180 form to specific addresses for older records.
- Requesting records through FOIA/Privacy Act requests to the VA for certain periods.
- Requesting online from NPRC for older records².

Additionally, active duty service members who have filed a BDD claim can view their Compensation & Pension exam reports via MHS Genesis under medical records > documents about 30 days after the exam³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Military Personnel Records - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)  
² [Service Treatment Records (STRs)/Military Medical Records - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)  
³ [Viewing Exam Reports - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Military Personnel Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)
- [Service Treatment Records (STRs)/Military Medical Records](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/infect)
- [Viewing Exam Reports](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Confidence:** 0.6034419536590576

### Question 11: CLAIMS-11

**Query:** Do I need a lawyer to file a claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.67s

**Answer:**

You do not need a lawyer to file a claim. You can file a claim yourself by completing the appropriate VA forms and submitting them as indicated. However, it is strongly recommended to use a Veterans Service Officer (VSO) to help with the process, as they can assist you in filing and navigating the claim system¹⁵.

For federal tort claims specifically, it is highly recommended to seek an attorney who specializes in federal tort claims, but this is not a requirement for general VA disability claims⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 14: CLAIMS-14

**Query:** What evidence do I need for my claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 6.49s

**Answer:**

For your VA disability claim, you need evidence that supports three essential elements for a successful claim:

1. **A current diagnosis** of a chronic disability.  
2. **An in-service event, exposure, or service-connected disability** that caused or worsened your disability.  
3. **A medical opinion (nexus statement)** connecting the current diagnosis to the in-service event or exposure¹.

You do not necessarily need to have all three elements fully developed before filing, but having at least the diagnosis and evidence of an in-service event will increase your chances of success¹.

Regarding the types of evidence you can submit:

- Medical records showing diagnosis and treatment of your claimed condition. This can include private medical records, non-VA hospital reports, and VA medical records (VA can obtain these themselves if you provide authorization)³.
- Buddy statements or personal statements describing how the condition occurred, worsened, or affects you³.
- Independent medical opinions or nexus letters connecting your disability to service².
- If applicable, supporting statements or specific forms for conditions like PTSD³.
- New and relevant evidence that was not previously considered, such as new diagnoses, new theories of service connection, or willingness to attend VA exams².

You can submit new evidence with your initial claim or within one year of a decision if appealing, and for supplemental claims, you have 90 days from the Notice of Disagreement to submit new evidence.

**Sources:**

¹ [Successful Claims](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
³ [Miscellaneous Evidence](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

**Sources Cited:**
- [2\. Evidence Submission](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)
- [Supplemental Claim [(20-0995)](http://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0995-ARE.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)
- [Miscellaneous Evidence](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 15: CLAIMS-15

**Query:** Can I file a claim while still on active duty?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.37s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can file a VA disability claim while still on active duty. If you have between 90 to 180 days before separation, you may file a Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) claim, which ideally allows you to get a claims decision the day after you separate. Even if you have less than 90 days before separation, you can and should still file a claim. You can file the claim yourself online at VA.gov or seek assistance from a Veterans Service Officer (VSO). It is also important to obtain your military medical records before separating from service and to document every issue you have had on your medical forms while still on active duty⁷.

**Sources:**
¹ [Active Duty Servicemembers - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
² [Filing a Claim - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
³ [Filing a BDD Claim - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 16: CLAIMS-16

**Query:** What is BDD (Benefits Delivery at Discharge)?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 5.03s

**Answer:**

Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) is a VA program that allows active duty servicemembers who are between 180 and 90 days before separation to file a disability claim within that window. The goal of BDD is to get a head start on the claim process so that the veteran can receive a decision on their claim the day after they separate from active duty. However, this outcome is not guaranteed.

Key points about BDD include:

- You must submit your Service Treatment Records (STRs) no later than 90 days before separation to remain in the BDD program.
- Private medical records are generally not included in STRs unless you arrange to have them added.
- You do not need to attend all your exams before the window closes or before separation.
- It is important to keep the VA updated on your location for proper notification of examinations.
- If you have less than 90 days before separation, you can and should still file a claim, though it may not be under the BDD program.
- Filing can be done online via VA.gov or with the assistance of a Veterans Service Officer (VSO).

BDD is designed to facilitate a smoother transition from active duty to veteran status by expediting the disability claims process¹⁴².

¹ [Benefits Delivery at Discharge (BDD) - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/digsystem)

⁴ [Active Duty Servicemembers - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 17: CLAIMS-17

**Query:** How do I file for an increase?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 2.89s

**Answer:**

To file for an increase in your VA disability rating, you can follow these steps:

- Review the VA Rating Schedule to see if your current disability level qualifies for an increase.
- File your increase claim on the va.gov website.
- The VA will then order new exam(s) to assess the current severity of your condition(s).
- You do not need to supply evidence that your condition has worsened; the VA will determine this through their exams.
- Be aware that filing for an increase opens your entire claims file for review, which can lead to corrections of any errors in your file. However, most raters focus only on the condition(s) you are requesting an increase for unless there is a clear error elsewhere.

¹

### Question 18: CLAIMS-18

**Query:** What is a supplemental claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 7.39s

**Answer:**

A supplemental claim is an opportunity to submit new and relevant evidence to the VA to support a previously denied claim. "New" evidence means the VA did not previously have or consider it, and "relevant" evidence directly addresses the rationale for the denial. Examples of new and relevant evidence include medical records, independent medical opinions, personal statements, buddy statements, or a new theory of service connection. Veterans have one year from the claim's decision to file a supplemental claim to potentially keep their back pay. It is recommended to wait until you have enough evidence before filing¹.

You can submit a supplemental claim through various methods, including online forms, in-person via a Veterans Service Organization (VSO) or lawyer, or by mail. However, the VA uses the date the appeal is processed, not the postmark date, to determine timeliness, so timely submission is critical².

The supplemental claim process involves a review of the new evidence by a VA reviewer rather than a rater, which may take longer. You can submit additional evidence within 90 days after your Notice of Disagreement (NOD) is received³.

The status of a supplemental claim typically shows as either "We don't know your status" or "A reviewer is examining your new evidence" until the claim is closed out⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)

³ [2. Evidence Submission - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
⁴ [Supplemental Claim Status - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)

### Question 20: CLAIMS-20

**Query:** Can I reopen a denied claim?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 6.66s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.2%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

Yes, you can reopen a denied claim by filing a Supplemental Claim (VA Form 20-0995). This allows you to submit new and relevant evidence that was not previously considered by the VA. New evidence can include things like new medical records, an independent medical opinion (IMO) or nexus letter, personal statements, buddy statements, or a new theory of service connection. It is important that the evidence directly addresses the reason for the denial, such as providing a diagnosis if that was missing before. You have one year from the date of the decision to file a Supplemental Claim to potentially keep your back pay. If you were previously denied because you missed a VA exam, you can state on the Supplemental Claim form that you are now willing and able to attend the exam¹.

If your claim was denied due to lack of service connection, you should determine what was missing—such as a current diagnosis, an in-service event, or a medical opinion linking the condition to service. If you believe the VA did not properly evaluate your evidence, a Higher-Level Review (HLR) might be appropriate. Otherwise, submitting additional supporting evidence through a Supplemental Claim is recommended².

Do not file a new claim for the same or a similar condition that was previously denied, as it will be rejected and you will be directed to appeal instead. Appeals include Supplemental Claims, Higher-Level Reviews, or Board Appeals³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
² [Denied Service-Connection - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
³ [BEFORE Filing a Claim for a New Disability - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 23: CLAIMS-23

**Query:** How do I prove service connection?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.78s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** Some specific numbers in this response could not be verified against my sources. Please verify key statistics with official VA documentation.

To prove service connection for a disability claim, you need to establish the following:

- A current diagnosis of the disability.
- An in-service event, injury, or disease that could have caused or contributed to the disability.
- Medical evidence or an examiner's opinion that shows it is at least as likely as not (50% or greater probability) that the disability is related to your military service or a service-connected condition.

If your claim was denied because these elements were missing or the examiner concluded there was a "less likely than not" chance your disability was service-connected, you can take further steps such as:

- Submitting additional supporting evidence, like an Independent Medical Opinion (IMO) or Nexus letter.
- Providing personal statements or buddy statements to support your claim.
- Requesting a Higher-Level Review (HLR) if you believe the evidence was not properly evaluated.
- Filing a supplemental claim if you need to submit new evidence or if you missed a VA exam, stating your willingness and ability to attend exams.

These steps can help strengthen your claim to prove service connection¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Denied Service-Connection - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Denied Service-Connection](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 25: CLAIMS-25

**Query:** Can I file a claim years after discharge?

**Category:** CLAIMS

**Response Time:** 4.17s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can file a claim years after discharge. There is no information in the provided context that limits the time frame for filing a new disability claim after discharge. However, if you have been previously denied for a condition or want to claim a similar condition, you should not file a new claim but instead submit an appropriate appeal¹. 

Additionally, if you are filing for accrued benefits as a dependent after the Veteran's death, there is a one-year time limit to act from the date of the Veteran's death or the claim decision².

But for general disability claims, the context does not specify a strict deadline for filing after discharge.

### Question 26: APPEAL-1

**Query:** How do I appeal a VA decision?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 11.78s

**Answer:**

To appeal a VA decision, you have three initial methods to choose from:

1. Higher-Level Review (HLR)
2. Supplemental Claim
3. Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA)

Key points to consider when appealing:

- You must file your appeal within one year of your claim's decision date to keep your effective date (back pay). Missing this deadline means you can only get your original effective date if there is a Clear and Unmistakable Error (CUE)¹.
- You cannot file multiple appeals for the same issue at the same time without first withdrawing the current appeal¹.
- If you filed an appeal to the BVA and are waiting, you cannot file a supplemental claim for the same issues while the BVA case is pending¹.
- Lawyers and claims agents can only charge up to 33.3% of your back pay and only get paid if you win your appeal. VSOs are free resources¹.
- Before appealing, carefully read your decision letter to understand what evidence was considered, favorable findings, missing information, or exams required⁴.

Submitting your appeal:

- For Higher-Level Review, Supplemental Claim, or BVA, you can submit online, via your VSO or lawyer, by mail, or fax (for BVA)² ⁵ ⁶.
- For BVA appeals, you must submit within one year of your claim's decision, and you cannot request another BVA appeal for the same issue immediately after a BVA decision³.
- BVA appeals are reviewed by Veterans Law Judges who can make decisions independent of VA Central Office policies³.

In summary:

- Choose your appeal method (HLR, Supplemental Claim, or BVA).
- File within one year of the decision date.
- Submit your appeal through the appropriate channels.
- Use VSOs or lawyers for assistance if desired.
- Follow instructions in your decision letter to strengthen your appeal.

**Sources:**

¹ [Filing an Appeal](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
² [Submitting to BVA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
³ [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA 10182](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
⁴ [BEFORE You File an Appeal](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
⁵ [Submitting an HLR](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
⁶ [Submitting a Supplemental Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 27: APPEAL-2

**Query:** What is a Higher Level Review?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 4.1s

**Answer:**

A Higher-Level Review (HLR) is an entirely new review of your claim conducted by an experienced, more senior rater known as a Decision Review Officer (DRO). This review involves a fresh look at the evidence already of record but does **not** allow the submission or consideration of any new evidence received after the original decision was made. The DRO has the authority to issue differences of opinion, which means they can weigh the existing evidence differently to potentially reach a more favorable outcome for the veteran. However, differences of opinion cannot result in a negative outcome for the veteran. If there were Clear and Unmistakable Errors (CUEs) in the previous decision, those can be corrected even if the correction results in a negative outcome, provided appropriate due notice is given.

Additional important points about Higher-Level Reviews include:

- Veterans cannot file an HLR consecutively (back-to-back) but can file an HLR into a supplemental claim and so on.
- Veterans cannot request an HLR after a Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA) denial.
- The HLR must be submitted within one year of the claim's decision.
- New evidence cannot be submitted or considered during an HLR.

In summary, an HLR is a senior rater's review of the existing evidence in a claim to potentially correct or improve the decision without introducing new evidence¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Higher-Level Review (HLR 20-0996)](https://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0996-ARE.pdf)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Higher-Level Review [(HLR 20-0996)](https://www.vba.va.gov/pubs/forms/VBA-20-0996-ARE.pdf)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [1\. Direct Review](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [Ratersâ€™ Discretion (Rating Wiggle)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)
- [Low Rating](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 28: APPEAL-3

**Query:** What is a Supplemental Claim?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 6.48s

**Answer:**

A Supplemental Claim is an opportunity for a veteran to submit **new and relevant evidence** to the VA to support their claim. "New" means the VA did not previously have or consider the evidence, and "relevant" means the evidence directly addresses the reason for the prior denial. Examples of new and relevant evidence include medical records, new theories of service connection, independent medical opinions, personal or buddy statements, or willingness to attend a new examination. Veterans have **1 year** from the decision on their claim to file a Supplemental Claim to potentially preserve their effective date for back pay¹.

There are several ways to submit a Supplemental Claim, including online, by paper form, through a Veterans Service Organization (VSO) or lawyer, in person at a VA Regional Office, or by mail. It is important to note that the VA uses the date the appeal is processed, not the postmark date, to determine timeliness, so late processing can result in loss of the effective date².

A Supplemental Claim is reviewed by a VA rater who considers the new evidence submitted. The claim status may show as "We don't know your status" or "A reviewer is examining your new evidence" until the claim is resolved⁴.

### Question 29: APPEAL-4

**Query:** What is the Board of Veterans Appeals?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.47s

**Answer:**

The Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA) is a body where Veterans Law Judges review appeals from veterans regarding their VA claims decisions. These judges are not bound by VA Central Office policy decisions and have the authority to issue grants that VA raters cannot. The BVA appeals process can take years to complete and is typically chosen after other appeal options like Supplemental Claim or Higher Level Review have been exhausted. Veterans must submit their appeal to the BVA within one year of their claim's decision and cannot request a BVA appeal after this time frame or consecutively for the same claimed item. When appealing to the BVA, veterans can choose among three lanes or appeal dockets: Direct Review, Evidence Submission, or Hearing¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA 10182](https://www.va.gov/vaforms/va/pdf/VA10182.pdf)

**Sources Cited:**

**Answer:**

The length of time an appeal takes can vary depending on the type of appeal chosen. Specifically, appeals to the Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA) are known to take years to complete. ### Question 32: APPEAL-7

**Query:** Can I get a hearing for my appeal?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.02s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get a hearing for your appeal. The hearing allows you to plead your case before a judge either by videoconference or in-person in Washington, DC. After the hearing, you will have 90 days to submit any new evidence. The judge will then make a decision considering both the hearing and the evidence of record¹.

### Question 33: APPEAL-8

**Query:** What is a Board hearing?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.12s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.9%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

A Board hearing is an opportunity for a veteran to present their case before a Veterans Law Judge either by videoconference or in-person in Washington, DC. After the hearing, the veteran has 90 days to submit any new evidence. The judge will then make a decision based on the hearing and the evidence of record¹. This hearing is one of the appeal lanes available when appealing to the Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA)².

### Question 34: APPEAL-9

**Query:** Should I get a lawyer for my appeal?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.1s

**Answer:**

You may choose to get a lawyer for your appeal, but it is not required. You can also use the assistance of a Veterans Service Organization (VSO) or an accredited claims agent. VSOs provide free assistance to veterans, while lawyers and claims agents can charge up to 33.3% of your back pay (excluding expenses) and only get paid if you win your appeal. This means you have options depending on your preference and resources.

### Question 35: APPEAL-10

**Query:** What happens if I miss the appeal deadline?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 2.07s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **47.7%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

If you miss the appeal deadline, which is within one year of your claim's decision date, you generally cannot keep your original effective date (your back pay). Veterans who fail to file an appeal within this one-year period can only get their original effective date and back pay if there is a Clear and Unmistakable Error (CUE) in the decision¹.

### Question 38: APPEAL-13

**Query:** Can I submit new evidence on appeal?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 6.56s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can submit new evidence on appeal, but it depends on the type of appeal:

- For a **Supplemental Claim**, you can submit **new and relevant** evidence. "New" means the VA did not previously have or consider it, and "relevant" means it directly addresses the rationale for the denial¹.

- For an appeal involving a **hearing**, after your hearing you have 90 days to submit any new evidence. The judge will consider the hearing and the evidence of record².

- For an appeal reviewed by a judge (such as a Board of Veterans' Appeals appeal), you can submit new evidence within 90 days from the day your Notice of Disagreement (NOD) is received³.

- However, during an **Informal Conference** or when submitting a **Personal Statement** as an alternative to an informal conference, you **cannot** add new evidence; you can only discuss errors of fact or law related to your appeal⁴⁵.

In summary, submitting new evidence is allowed in supplemental claims and hearings with time limits, but not during informal conferences or personal statements on appeal.

**Sources:**
¹ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
² [3. Hearing - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)  
³ [2. Evidence Submission - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)  
⁴ [Informal Conference - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
⁵ [Alternative to Informal Conference - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 40: APPEAL-15

**Query:** How do I choose an appeal lane?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 6.45s

**Answer:**

When choosing an appeal lane for your VA claim, you have three initial methods to appeal:

- Higher-Level Review  
- Supplemental Claim  
- Board of Veterans Appeals (BVA)

You must pick one lane and cannot file multiple appeals for the same issue at the same time without first withdrawing the current appeal.

If you choose the BVA lane, there are three dockets (lanes) you can select from:

1. Direct Review  
2. Evidence Submission  
3. Hearing

The BVA option is usually chosen after the other appeal options have been exhausted, as it can take years to complete³.

Remember, you must file your appeal within one year of your claim's decision date to keep your effective date and back pay³.

**Sources:**  
¹ [Filing an Appeal - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
² [BEFORE You File an Appeal - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
³ [Board Veterans' Appeals (BVA) AKA, Notice of Disagreement (NOD), AKA 10182 - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 41: APPEAL-16

**Query:** What is the Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 3.53s

**Answer:**

The Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims (CAVC) is a judicial body that interprets the laws and regulations governing the Veterans Benefits Administration (VBA). The judges at the CAVC have broad authority to interpret these laws and regulations, and their decisions create case law that can change how the VBA operates. Veterans have 120 days after receiving a Board of Veterans' Appeals (BVA) decision to file an appeal with the CAVC. This appeal must be submitted directly to the CAVC and not to the VA. While veterans can represent themselves, it is strongly recommended to seek legal representation at this stage¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Court of Appeals for Veterans Claims (CAVC) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 43: APPEAL-18

**Query:** What if my appeal is denied?

**Category:** APPEAL

**Response Time:** 7.2s

**Answer:**

If your appeal is denied, you can try another eligible appeal option. The three initial methods to appeal are:

1. Higher-Level Review (HLR)
2. Supplemental claim
3. Board of Veteran Appeals (BVA)

If one method is denied, you can file an appeal using a different method, but you cannot file multiple appeals for the same issue simultaneously without first withdrawing the current appeal.

Additionally, if you want to keep your effective date (your back pay), you must file an appeal within one year of your claim's decision date. If you miss this deadline, you can only get your original effective date and back pay if there is a Clear and Unmistakable Error (CUE).

If your claim was denied service connection, consider whether you were missing a current diagnosis, an in-service event, or if the examiner found it less likely than not that your disability was related to service. If you believe the rater did not properly evaluate the evidence, a Higher-Level Review may be appropriate. If you need to submit additional supporting information, a supplemental claim may be appropriate. You can submit new and relevant evidence such as independent medical opinions, personal statements, or buddy statements. If you missed an exam, you can file a supplemental claim stating you are willing and able to attend exams¹⁴⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [Filing an Appeal - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
² [BEFORE You File an Appeal - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
⁴ [Denied Service-Connection - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
⁵ [Supplemental Claim (20-0995) - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals#995)

**Sources Cited:**
- [BEFORE You File an Appeal](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 46: RATING-1

**Query:** How are VA disability ratings calculated?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 7.13s

**Answer:**

VA disability ratings are calculated based on the severity of the Veteran's service-connected disabilities as determined by the Schedule for Rating Disabilities used by the Veterans Benefits Administration (VBA). The rating percentage reflects how much the disability decreases the Veteran's overall health and ability to function.

For mental health conditions, the rating is determined by evaluating the totality of symptoms and evidence submitted, including C&P examiner reports, Disability Benefits Questionnaires (DBQs), nexus letters, buddy letters, personal statements, treatment notes, and other supporting documents. The rater assigns a percentage that most closely matches the Veteran's overall symptom picture rather than requiring every single symptom to be present¹.

The VBA uses diagnostic codes to identify specific conditions and applies the rating criteria in the Schedule for Rating Disabilities to assign a disability percentage. This percentage corresponds to the severity and impact of the condition on the Veteran's daily life and employability¹⁴.

It is important to provide comprehensive and accurate evidence to demonstrate the severity of the disability, as misrepresenting the condition is a crime. The rating schedule should be used to understand how the VA evaluates disabilities, not as a checklist to meet every criterion⁴⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [How a Rater Decides](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁴ [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)  
⁵ [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Common VA Diagnostic Codes Reference](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [70%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/combinedbenefits)
- [How a Rater Decides](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)
- [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [The Rating Schedule Index](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [VA Disability](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/smc)
- [60%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/combinedbenefits#top)

**Confidence:** 0.6079373359680176
**Chunks Retrieved:** 7

### Question 47: RATING-2

**Query:** What is combined rating?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 2.48s

**Answer:**

A combined rating refers to the total disability rating a Veteran receives when multiple service-connected conditions are evaluated together. The combined rating reflects the overall level of disability by considering all individual ratings for separate conditions. Importantly, a Veteran who is rated at a certain combined percentage (for example, 50%) receives all the federal benefits associated with that rating and all lower ratings as well. This means benefits accumulate as the combined rating increases¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Federal Benefits List by Combined Ratings - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/combinedbenefits)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Federal Benefits List by Combined Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/combinedbenefits)
- [Single Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin)
- [Multiple Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/skin)
- [Miscellaneous Ratings](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [Miscellaneous Ratings](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)
- [General Rating Schedule](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)
- [Miscellaneous Ratings](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)

### Question 48: RATING-3

**Query:** How does VA math work?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.77s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **49.1%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

VA math, in the context of disability benefits, refers to how combined disability ratings are calculated and how they affect eligibility for certain benefits. For example, to qualify for Individual Unemployability, a veteran must have two or more service-connected disabilities with at least one rated at 40% or more, and a combined rating of 70% or more. This combined rating must result in the veteran being unable to hold down substantially gainful employment due to their service-connected conditions. Odd jobs or marginal employment do not count against this determination. Additionally, a 70% or higher combined disability rating can make a veteran eligible for certain health care programs, family caregiver assistance, and basic eligibility for VA nursing homes, among other benefits¹.

In education benefits, VA math involves proration of payments such as the housing allowance. Unlike VA disability payments, which are not prorated, education benefits like the Monthly Housing Allowance (MHA) can be prorated based on enrollment status and the number of days in a month. For instance, if a course starts partway through a month, the MHA is paid proportionally based on a 30-day calendar month.

**Sources:**
¹ [70% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/combinedbenefits)  
² [Frequently (Part 3) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vre#frequently)

### Question 50: RATING-5

**Query:** Can I get 100% disability?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 8.91s

**Answer:**

You may be eligible for a 100% disability rating temporarily if you are hospitalized or convalescing due to a service-connected condition. To file for temporary 100% disability, you can:

- Seek assistance from a Veterans Service Organization (VSO) or file online at VA.gov.
- File a new claim specifically for "temporary 100% due to hospitalization" or "temporary 100% due to convalescence."
- Provide medical evidence that clearly states:
  - The condition(s) causing the hospitalization or convalescence.
  - The length of the hospitalization or convalescence.
  - If related to surgery, additional details such as date of admission, date of discharge, date of surgery, procedure performed, and length of convalescence must be included.

If you are hospitalized at a VA facility, the VA should handle the claim for you, and you may not need to file it yourself⁶⁷.

For permanent 100% disability, the context does not provide specific criteria or information. You might want to consult the VA or a VSO for details about permanent 100% disability ratings.

### Question 51: RATING-6

**Query:** What is the difference between schedular and TDIU?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 8.24s

**Answer:**

The difference between schedular and TDIU (Total Disability based on Individual Unemployability) lies primarily in how the disability rating is determined and the veteran's ability to work:

- **Schedular Rating**: This is the disability rating assigned based on the VA's Schedule for Rating Disabilities, which evaluates the severity of a veteran's service-connected conditions according to specific criteria. It reflects the percentage of disability based on the severity of symptoms and impairment as outlined in the rating schedule¹⁴⁵.

- **TDIU (Total Disability based on Individual Unemployability)**: This is a benefit that allows veterans who are unable to maintain substantially gainful employment due to their service-connected disabilities to be compensated at the 100% disability rate, even if their schedular rating is less than 100%. To qualify for TDIU, a veteran must have at least one service-connected disability rated at 60% or higher and be unable to hold a steady job because of their service-connected disabilities. Marginal or odd jobs do not disqualify a veteran from TDIU eligibility.

In summary, schedular ratings are based on the severity of disabilities as per the rating schedule, while TDIU is granted when a veteran's disabilities prevent them from working, allowing for a 100% disability compensation regardless of the combined schedular rating.

**Sources:**
¹ [Unemployability - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)  
² [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/IU)  
⁴ [The Rating Schedule Index - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)  
⁵ [The Rating Schedule Index - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex)

### Question 53: RATING-8

**Query:** What is SMC (Special Monthly Compensation)?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 2.47s

**Answer:**

Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) is a higher level of monthly tax-free monetary benefit provided by the VA for veterans who have specific needs or disabilities. For example, if a veteran's traumatic brain injury (TBI) results in the need for regular aid and attendance, they may qualify for SMC. The amount of SMC can range up to over $10,000 per month depending on the severity and circumstances.

### Question 57: RATING-12

**Query:** Can my rating be reduced?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 8.68s

**Answer:**

Your VA disability rating can potentially be reduced, but there are protections in place to prevent reductions based on a single exam if your rating has been held for at least 5 continuous years. Specifically:

- A rating generally cannot be reduced based on just one Compensation & Pension (C&P) exam showing improvement because continuous improvement must be demonstrated over time if the evaluation has been held for 5 continuous years.
- Usually, the VA will require another exam confirming the continued improvement before issuing a proposal for reduction if the reduction would lower your combined evaluation.
- Some exceptions exist, such as conditions rated based on lab results (e.g., kidney or lung function), where a single lab result showing improvement may lead to a reduction, especially if you filed an increase claim.
- The 5-year protection does not reset after 5 years; even if improvement is shown at year 6 and again at year 16, the VA can still propose a reduction based on evidence of improvement.

So, while your rating can be reduced, there are safeguards to prevent abrupt reductions without clear and continuous evidence of improvement³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Low Rating - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)  
² [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)  
³ [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Low Rating](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/appeals)

### Question 58: RATING-13

**Query:** What is a protected rating?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.8s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.3%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

A protected rating in the context of VA disability benefits refers to a rating percentage that is safeguarded from reduction for a certain period of time after it has been assigned. For example, if a veteran initially receives a 10% rating for a condition, that 10% rating is protected for 5 years. If the rating is later increased (e.g., to 30%), the new increased rating does not immediately receive the same 5-year protection. However, the original 10% rating retains its 5-year protection and even a 20-year protection if it has been held for that long. This means the veteran cannot have the original rating reduced during those protected periods, even if the condition changes or is reevaluated.

In summary:
- The 5-year protection applies only to the original rating percentage, not to the increased rating.
- The original rating can also have a 20-year protection if it has been held for 20 years.
- The increased rating does not automatically get the same protection as the original rating.

This protection ensures stability in the veteran's disability compensation for a specified time frame after the rating is assigned or increased.

¹ [Example Involving Increase - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

### Question 61: RATING-16

**Query:** What is an effective date?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.03s

**Answer:**

An effective date is the date from which VA benefits, such as compensation or Dependency and Indemnity Compensation (DIC), are calculated and paid. For 1151 claims, the effective date for compensation is either:

- The date of the injury or aggravation if the claim is received within one year of the incident; or
- The date the claim was initiated if more than one year has passed.

For DIC, the effective date is either:

- The first day of the month in which the Veteran’s death occurred if a claim is received within one year following the date of death; or
- The date the claim was received if more than one year has passed¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Effective Dates - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#effectivedates)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Effective Dates](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#effectivedates)

### Question 64: RATING-19

**Query:** What is P&T (Permanent and Total)?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 3.94s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **47.1%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

⚠️ **Note:** Some specific numbers in this response could not be verified against my sources. Please verify key statistics with official VA documentation.

P&T (Permanent and Total) refers to a disability rating status where a veteran is rated 100% disabled and this rating is considered both permanent and total. This status provides several benefits, including:

- Entitlement to Chapter 35 Dependents Educational Assistance (DEA).
- Removal of the 10% penalty for early withdrawal of 401K and IRAs.
- Eligibility for veteran's dependents to enroll in CHAMPVA.
- The ability for the veteran to use non-VA emergency rooms during a medical emergency with reimbursement claims submitted within 2 years.
- Expedited processing of the veteran's disability claims by Social Security.
- Free air travel on Space-A for the veteran and their dependents when accompanied by the veteran.

These benefits highlight the comprehensive support available to veterans with a 100% P&T rating¹.

### Question 65: RATING-20

**Query:** What is the 5 year rule?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 6.77s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **47.4%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

The 5 year rule refers to the protection a Veteran has against reduction of their disability rating based on a single Compensation & Pension (C&P) exam showing improvement. Specifically:

- A Veteran's rating generally cannot be reduced based on just one exam that shows improvement if the evaluation has been held for at least 5 continuous years.
- Continuous improvement must be shown before a reduction proposal can be issued.
- In most cases, the VA will require another exam confirming continued improvement before issuing a proposal for reduction if it affects the combined rating.
- There are exceptions, such as conditions rated based on lab results (e.g., kidney or lung function), where a single lab result showing improvement can lead to a reduction if the Veteran has filed for an increase and the VA confirms the improvement.
- This 5-year protection does not reset after 5 years. For example, if improvement was shown at year 6 and again at year 16, the VA can still seek to reduce the rating based on those findings.

In summary, the 5 year rule protects Veterans from rating reductions based on a single exam showing improvement if the rating has been stable for 5 continuous years, requiring continuous improvement to justify a reduction.

**Sources:**
¹ [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)  
² [5 Years - Reductions (38 CFR 3.344) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

### Question 66: RATING-21

**Query:** What is the 10 year rule?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 4.64s

**Answer:**

The 10 year rule refers to a protection under 38 CFR 3.957 that prevents the VA from severing a Veteran's service-connection for a disability after 10 years, except in cases of fraud or if it is clearly shown from military records that the Veteran did not have the required service or character of discharge. After 10 years:

- The Veteran's erroneously service-connected condition can still be increased if warranted.
- Disabilities caused by the erroneously service-connected condition can be service-connected on a secondary basis.
- The VA can reassign the location of a disability grant (e.g., left vs. right side) even after 10 years, but if this results in a reduced evaluation, the VA must provide due process and propose the change.
- Periods when the Veteran is returned to Active Duty do not count toward the 10-year calculation.

This rule essentially protects the Veteran's service-connection from being removed after 10 years, with some specific exceptions and provisions for adjustments.

**Sources:**
¹ [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)  
² [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957) - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

**Sources Cited:**
- [10 Years - Service-Connection (38 CFR 3.957)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

### Question 67: RATING-22

**Query:** What is the 20 year rule?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 5.07s

**Answer:**

The 20 year rule refers to a protection under 38 CFR 3.951 that prevents a Veteran's entitlement to a benefit or rating from being severed or reduced after 20 years, except in cases of fraud. This means once a Veteran has held a benefit or rating for 20 years, it generally cannot be lowered or taken away.

Special notes related to this rule include:
- If the VA erroneously granted service-connection for a disability involving a specific location (such as left vs. right side or upper vs. lower), the VA can reassign the location even after 20 years. If this reassignment results in a reduced evaluation, the VA must provide due process and propose the change.
- Periods when a Veteran returns to Active Duty do not count toward the calculation of this 20-year protection.
- Special Monthly Compensation (SMC) based on need for aid and attendance can be paused if the Veteran is hospitalized at government expense.

**Sources:**
¹ [20 Years - Total Protection (38 CFR 3.951)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)  
² [20 Years - Total Protection (38 CFR 3.951)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

**Sources Cited:**

- [20 Years - Total Protection (38 CFR 3.951)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ratingsindex#protection)

### Question 69: RATING-24

**Query:** What is individual unemployability?

**Category:** RATING

**Response Time:** 2.58s

**Answer:**

Individual Unemployability, also known as Total Disability based on Individual Unemployability (TDIU), is a benefit that Veterans can apply for if their service-connected disabilities, such as Traumatic Brain Injury (TBI) or others, make them unable to maintain gainful employment. This allows Veterans to receive compensation at the 100% disability rate even if their combined disability rating is less than 100%¹.

**Category:** RATING

### Question 71: MENTAL-1

**Query:** How is PTSD rated by the VA?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 10.61s

**Answer:**

PTSD (Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder) is rated by the VA as a mental health disorder based on the severity of the totality of the Veteran's symptoms, not on the formal diagnosis itself. A Veteran with PTSD receives a single combined mental health rating regardless of the number of mental health diagnoses they have¹ ⁷.

For service-connection, PTSD requires an in-service stressor, which can include a variety of traumatic events such as combat exposure, assault, military sexual trauma, environmental disasters, and other specified stressors. However, anticipation of future deployment or learning of a death away from the Veteran in a hostile area does not qualify as an in-service stressor³.

The VA uses specific symptom criteria to determine the rating percentage. For example, a 50% rating corresponds to occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as panic attacks more than once a week, difficulty understanding complex commands, impaired judgment, disturbances of mood, and difficulty maintaining effective work and social relationships².

Additionally, PTSD and Traumatic Brain Injury (TBI) symptoms often overlap, and the VA may lump these ratings together unless the symptoms are clearly separable, in which case separate ratings can be assigned⁴.

Ultimately, the rating decision is based on the totality of symptoms and evidence, including C&P examiner reports, DBQs, personal statements, and other supporting documentation⁵.

**Sources:**
¹ [Mental Disorder Ratings - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [50% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
³ [PTSD Service-Connection - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
⁴ [PTSD & TBI - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
⁵ [How a Rater Decides - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)  
⁷ [Mental Disorders - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 72: MENTAL-2

**Query:** What symptoms qualify for PTSD rating?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 5.2s

**Answer:**

The symptoms that qualify for a PTSD rating include:

- Flattened affect  
- Circumstantial, circumlocutory, or stereotyped speech  
- Panic attacks more than once a week  
- Difficulty in understanding complex commands  
- Impairment of short- and long-term memory (e.g., retention of only highly learned material, forgetting to complete tasks)  
- Impaired judgment  
- Impaired abstract thinking  
- Disturbances of motivation and mood  
- Difficulty in establishing and maintaining effective work and social relationships  
- Extreme lack of motivation and/or mood swings  
- Difficulty expressing themselves or showing emotions, or responding with inappropriate emotions  
- Constant signs of severe anxiety  
- Difficulty thinking logically and making poor judgments  

Additionally, the Veteran may require a fiduciary due to these impairments. The Veteran's ability to care for themselves may be mostly intact but hygiene may not always be maintained, and they may occasionally be hospitalized. They may be able to work but have difficulty maintaining employment for long periods, and social relationships may be short-lived due to inability to be an active participant¹.

Also, for service-connection of PTSD, an in-service stressor is required, which can include a wide range of traumatic events such as combat-related fear, assault, military sexual trauma, accidents, and others².

### Question 73: MENTAL-3

**Query:** Can I get service connected for PTSD without combat?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 4.83s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get service-connected for PTSD without direct combat exposure. PTSD service-connection requires an in-service stressor, but this stressor does not have to be combat-related. Examples of non-combat stressors include:

- Aircraft crashes  
- Assault or battery  
- Environmental disasters (earthquakes, fires, floods, etc.)  
- Harassment or military sexual trauma (MST)  
- Sexual assault or harassment  
- Serving as a drone aircraft crew member  
- Serving in mortuary affairs  
- Stalking  
- Training or vehicular accidents  

Fear of hostile enemy forces is also a qualifying stressor, but it does not include anticipation of future deployment or learning of a death away from the veteran in hostile locations¹.

**Sources:**  
¹ [PTSD Service-Connection - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 77: MENTAL-7

**Query:** What is the 100% rating for mental health?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 4.94s

**Answer:**

The 100% rating for mental health indicates total occupational and social impairment due to symptoms such as:

- Gross impairment in thought processes or communication
- Persistent delusions or hallucinations
- Grossly inappropriate behavior
- Persistent danger of hurting self or others
- Intermittent inability to perform activities of daily living (including maintenance of minimal personal hygiene)
- Disorientation to time or place
- Memory loss for names of close relatives, own occupation, or own name

Additional details include:

- The Veteran is unable to care for themselves and may require constant or near-constant hospitalization due to being a threat to themselves or others.
- The Veteran has no ability to work (except possibly in a protected environment).
- Social relationships, if they exist, are one-sided and directed toward the Veteran; the Veteran does not seek or encourage social interaction.
- Symptoms may include constant risk to self or others (including suicidal tendencies), significant memory loss, severe anxiety, fear, paranoia, inappropriate behavior (e.g., drooling, mumbling, shouting), inability to differentiate reality, and inability to communicate, reason, or think logically¹.

**Sources:**

### Question 78: MENTAL-8

**Query:** Can I have multiple mental health ratings?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 3.83s

**Answer:**

No, you cannot have multiple mental health ratings. Veterans can only have one mental health rating that combines the symptoms of all their mental disorders. The only exception is if the Veteran has an eating disorder, in which case they will receive one rating for their mental health disorders and a separate rating for the eating disorder¹³.

### Question 79: MENTAL-9

**Query:** How is anxiety rated?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 8.8s

**Answer:**

Anxiety is rated based on the severity of symptoms and the impact on occupational and social functioning. The rating does not depend on the specific diagnosis but on the overall severity of symptoms. Here are some key rating levels for anxiety and related mental disorders:

- **10% rating**: Occupational and social impairment due to mild or transient symptoms which decrease work efficiency and ability to perform occupational tasks only during periods of significant stress, or symptoms controlled by continuous medication. Symptoms include mild anxiety, depression, or mood changes; rare mild panic attacks; occasional sleep disturbances. The veteran is always able to care for themselves and rarely has problems working. Social relationships may have occasional issues¹³.

- **30% rating**: Occupational and social impairment with occasional decrease in work efficiency and intermittent periods of inability to perform occupational tasks. Symptoms may include depressed mood, anxiety, suspiciousness, panic attacks (weekly or less often), chronic sleep impairment, and mild memory loss. The veteran can usually take care of themselves but may occasionally be hospitalized. Work ability is occasionally impaired, and social relationships may be strained¹⁵.

- **50% rating**: Occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as flattened affect, panic attacks more than once a week, difficulty understanding complex commands, impaired memory and judgment, disturbances of motivation and mood, and difficulty maintaining effective work and social relationships. The veteran is mostly able to care for themselves but hygiene may not always be maintained. Work may be possible but difficult to sustain long term. Social relationships may be short-lived¹⁶.

The rating is based on the totality of symptoms rather than the specific anxiety diagnosis, and a veteran receives a single rating regardless of the number of diagnoses¹⁴.

**Sources:**

- [Mental Disorder Ratings](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [30%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [50%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 80: MENTAL-10

**Query:** How is depression rated?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 8.94s

**Answer:**

Depression, like other mental health disorders, is rated based on the severity of the totality of the Veteran's symptoms rather than the specific diagnosis itself. The Veteran receives a single rating regardless of the number of formal diagnoses they have. The rating depends on how severe the symptoms are and how much they impair occupational and social functioning¹.

For example, a 50% rating corresponds to occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as:

- Flattened affect
- Panic attacks more than once a week
- Difficulty understanding complex commands
- Impairment of short- and long-term memory
- Impaired judgment and abstract thinking
- Disturbances of motivation and mood
- Difficulty establishing and maintaining effective work and social relationships

At this level, the Veteran is mostly able to care for themselves and perform daily tasks but may have difficulty maintaining long-term employment and relationships. Symptoms may include extreme lack of motivation, mood swings, severe anxiety, and multiple panic attacks per week².

Ultimately, the rating is determined by the rater based on the totality of symptoms and all evidence submitted, including medical examinations, treatment records, and personal statements³.

### Question 81: MENTAL-11

**Query:** What is occupational and social impairment?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 4.74s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **45.2%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

Occupational and social impairment refers to the degree to which a veteran's mental health symptoms affect their ability to work and interact socially. It ranges from mild or transient symptoms that only occasionally decrease work efficiency and social interactions, to total impairment where the veteran is unable to work or maintain social relationships due to severe symptoms.

- Mild occupational and social impairment involves symptoms such as mild anxiety or depression that decrease work efficiency only during significant stress, occasional social issues, and symptoms controlled by medication. The veteran is usually able to care for themselves and rarely has problems working¹.

- Total occupational and social impairment involves severe symptoms like persistent delusions or hallucinations, grossly inappropriate behavior, constant risk of harm to self or others, inability to perform daily living activities, disorientation, and memory loss. The veteran is unable to care for themselves, cannot work, and does not engage in social relationships except in a one-way manner directed toward them².

**Sources:**
¹ [10% - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [100% - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

**Sources Cited:**
- [10%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [100%](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

**Confidence:** 0.4520363509654999

### Question 83: MENTAL-13

**Query:** Do I need a diagnosis for PTSD claim?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 2.29s

**Answer:**

You do not need to have a diagnosis to claim PTSD or any other condition when filing a claim. According to the information on filing a claim, you do not need to have a diagnosis to submit a claim for disability benefits¹. However, for PTSD specifically, there are additional requirements for service-connection, such as having an in-service stressor, but a diagnosis itself is not a prerequisite to file the claim².

### Question 84: MENTAL-14

**Query:** Can I get PTSD rating for non-combat trauma?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 4.13s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get a PTSD rating for non-combat trauma. PTSD service-connection requires an in-service stressor, but this stressor does not have to be direct combat exposure. Examples of non-combat trauma that can qualify include:

- Aircraft crashes  
- Assault  
- Battery  
- Environmental disasters (earthquakes, fires, floods, etc)  
- Harassment  
- Military Sexual Trauma (MST)  
- Sexual assault  
- Sexual harassment  
- Rape  
- Mugging  
- Robbery  
- Serving as a Drone Aircraft Crew Member  
- Serving in Mortuary Affairs  
- Stalking  
- Training accidents  
- Vehicular crashes  

Fear of hostile enemy forces is also included, but it specifically excludes anticipation of future deployment or learning of a death away from the veteran in a hostile location¹.

**Sources:**

### Question 85: MENTAL-15

**Query:** What evidence do I need for mental health claim?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 5.12s

**Answer:**

For a mental health claim, you need to provide the following evidence:

- Private medical records and non-VA hospital reports related to your claimed mental health condition or showing that your disability has worsened. If you want the VA to obtain these records, you must provide VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a.
- Newspaper articles that show you were personally involved in an event or that an event occurred during a time you were in a specific location.
- Buddy statements from family members, friends, clergy, law enforcement personnel, or fellow service members who can provide information about your claimed condition, how and when it happened, how it worsened, and how it currently affects you.
- Supporting or personal statements to highlight important details in your records or to add context and detail that may not be otherwise known.
- For PTSD claims specifically, VA Form 21-0781 (Supporting Statements for Service-connection for PTSD) is generally required.

Additionally, the VA can access your VHA medical records themselves, so it is not necessary to upload them as part of your claim, but you may choose to do so to assist the process.

### Question 86: MENTAL-16

**Query:** How do I prove my PTSD stressor?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 8.43s

**Answer:**

To prove your PTSD stressor for VA disability claims, you need to provide evidence that verifies the in-service stressor that caused your PTSD. The VA looks at various types of evidence to verify these stressors, including:

- After action reports (AARs)
- Buddy Letters (statements from people who witnessed or have knowledge of the event)
- Combat awards, badges, decorations on your DD-214
- Command chronologies and war diaries
- Contemporaneous letters and diaries
- Daily staff journals
- Former prisoner of war documentation
- Personal Statements (your own detailed account)
- Police or insurance reports
- Private medical records
- Monthly summaries and morning reports
- Muster rolls
- Newspaper accounts of the traumatic event
- Operational reports and lessons learned
- Radio logs, deck logs, and ship histories
- Service treatment records (STRs) or service personnel records
- Supporting Statements for Service Connection for PTSD (VA Form 21-0781)
- Unit and organizational histories

Additionally, when filing for PTSD, the Supporting Statements for Service Connection for PTSD (VA Form 21-0781) is generally required to help establish the stressor¹.

If the trauma was not reported at the time, the VA may also look for "markers" of trauma in your records, such as changes in behavior, performance, medical treatment, or substance abuse around the time of the claimed trauma².

Remember, PTSD stressors can include a wide range of events, not just combat, such as assault, military sexual trauma, accidents, fear of hostile enemy forces, and others³.

**Sources:**

¹ [Evidence that VA looks at to Verify In-Service Stressors](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 87: MENTAL-17

**Query:** Can I get 100% for PTSD alone?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 6.66s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can receive a 100% disability rating for PTSD alone if your symptoms cause total occupational and social impairment. According to the criteria for a 100% rating, this includes symptoms such as:

- Gross impairment in thought processes or communication
- Persistent delusions or hallucinations
- Grossly inappropriate behavior
- Persistent danger of hurting self or others
- Intermittent inability to perform activities of daily living (including maintaining minimal personal hygiene)
- Disorientation to time or place
- Memory loss for names of close relatives, own occupation, or own name

Additionally, the veteran would be unable to care for themselves, require constant or near-constant hospitalization, have no ability to work (except possibly in a protected environment), and have severely impaired social relationships that are one-sided or non-participatory. Symptoms may also include constant severe anxiety, fear, paranoia, inappropriate behavior, inability to differentiate reality, and inability to communicate or think logically¹.

Therefore, if your PTSD symptoms meet these severe criteria, you can be rated at 100% for PTSD alone.

**Sources:**
¹ [100% - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 88: MENTAL-18

**Query:** What is the difference between 50% and 70% PTSD?

**Category:** MENTAL

**Response Time:** 8.9s

**Answer:**

The context provided includes detailed criteria for a 50% PTSD rating but does not explicitly provide the criteria for a 70% PTSD rating. Therefore, I can only describe the 50% rating and note the lack of specific information on the 70% rating.

**50% PTSD Rating Criteria:**

- Occupational and social impairment with reduced reliability and productivity due to symptoms such as:
  - Flattened affect
  - Circumstantial, circumlocutory, or stereotyped speech
  - Panic attacks more than once a week
  - Difficulty understanding complex commands
  - Impairment of short- and long-term memory (e.g., retention of only highly learned material, forgetting to complete tasks)
  - Impaired judgment and abstract thinking
  - Disturbances of motivation and mood
  - Difficulty establishing and maintaining effective work and social relationships

- Ability to care for self:
  - Mostly able to take care of themselves; hygiene may not always be maintained
  - Able to do daily tasks such as driving, shopping, household chores
  - May occasionally be hospitalized

- Ability to work:
  - Can work but may have difficulty staying employed for long periods

- Social relationships:
  - May form relationships but they may be short-lived due to inability to be an active participant

- Symptoms include:
  - Extreme lack of motivation and/or mood swings
  - Difficulty expressing emotions or inappropriate emotional responses
  - Two or more panic attacks per week
  - Constant severe anxiety
  - Difficulty understanding complex directions
  - Difficulty thinking logically and poor judgment; may require a fiduciary

The 70% rating is mentioned in the TBI rating example but without detailed criteria or description of symptoms that differentiate it from the 50% rating.

**Summary:**

- The 50% rating reflects significant occupational and social impairment with specific symptoms and functional limitations as described above.
- The 70% rating criteria are not detailed in the provided context, so the exact differences cannot be stated based on the information given.
