### Question 174: VRE-14

**Query:** Can I use VR&E and GI Bill together?

**Category:** VRE

**Response Time:** 6.38s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can use VR&E (Veterans Readiness and Employment) and GI Bill education benefits (such as Chapter 30 Montgomery GI Bill or Chapter 33 Post-9/11 GI Bill) together. Here are some key points from the context:

- VR&E benefits may not exceed 48 months of use, and GI Bill benefits used before VR&E count against this 48-month limit. However, GI Bill eligibility months used can possibly be restored via Retroactive Induction, potentially allowing you to use the GI Bill again separately from VR&E benefits.

- It is possible for someone to use 48 months of VR&E benefits and then separately use 48 months of GI Bill benefits.

- When using VR&E, if you have at least one day of Post-9/11 GI Bill eligibility, you can receive Basic Allowance for Housing (BAH) at the E-5 with dependent rate for your location without using up your GI Bill entitlement by using VR&E.

This means VR&E and GI Bill benefits can be coordinated and used together, with some conditions and potential restoration options for GI Bill eligibility if previously used⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Duration of Program - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vre#durationofprogram)  
² [Other Benefits of VRE (Part 3) - (benefits)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vre#otherbenefitsofvre)  
³ [Other Benefits of VRE (Part 2) - (benefits)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vre#otherbenefitsofvre)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Other Benefits of VRE (Part 3)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vre#otherbenefitsofvre)

### Question 175: VRE-15

**Query:** How long can I use VR&E benefits?

**Category:** VRE

**Response Time:** 6.7s

**Answer:**

You can use Veterans Readiness and Employment (VR&E) benefits for up to 48 months. However, this limit may be extended if special conditions are met. 

Additional important points include:
- Time used under the GI Bill before VR&E counts against the 48-month limit, unless restored via Retroactive Induction.
- It is possible to use 48 months of VR&E benefits and separately use 48 months of GI Bill benefits.
- Certain children of Vietnam Veterans and Veterans with covered service in Korea have a maximum eligibility of 24 months instead of 48 months.

¹ [Duration of Program - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vre#durationofprogram)

**Sources:**
¹ [Duration of Program - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vre#durationofprogram)

### Question 176: CHAMPVA-1

**Query:** What is CHAMPVA?

**Category:** CHAMPVA

**Response Time:** 6.37s

**Answer:**

CHAMPVA, or the Civilian Health and Medical Program of the Department of Veterans Affairs, is a health care program that provides coverage to the spouse, widow(er), and children of a Veteran who meets certain eligibility criteria related to service-connected disabilities or death. It covers most medically and psychologically necessary health care services and supplies, excluding dental care unless the beneficiary also enrolls in the VA Dental Insurance Program (VADIP). CHAMPVA beneficiaries must not be eligible for TRICARE to qualify for this program. The program includes pharmacy benefits and has cost-sharing features such as an outpatient deductible and a patient cost share up to a catastrophic cap. Providers who accept CHAMPVA agree to accept the VA's allowable amount as payment in full³⁴⁵⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Civilian Health and Medical Program of the Department of Veterans Affairs (CHAMPVA) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/survivorbenefits#top)  
² [CHAMPVA - (overview)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva)  
³ [Additional Eligibility - (eligibility)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#additionaleligibilit)  
⁴ [Benefits - (benefits)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#benefits)  
⁵ [Eligibility - (eligibility)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#eligibility)  
⁶ [Frequently (Part 1) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#frequently)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Civilian Health and Medical Program of the Department of Veterans Affairs (CHAMPVA)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/survivorbenefits#top)

- [Additional Eligibility](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#additionaleligibilit)

### Question 177: CHAMPVA-2

**Query:** Who is eligible for CHAMPVA?

**Category:** CHAMPVA

**Response Time:** 5.38s

**Answer:**

CHAMPVA eligibility is available to the spouse or widow(er) and children of a Veteran who meets one of the following conditions:

- The Veteran is rated Permanently and Totally disabled due to service-connected condition(s);
- The Veteran was rated Permanently and Totally disabled due to service-connected condition(s) at the time of death;
- The Veteran died due to a service-connected condition; or
- The Veteran died on Active Duty and the dependents are not otherwise eligible for Department of Defense TRICARE benefits.

Additional important eligibility notes include:

- The beneficiary cannot be eligible for TRICARE.
- Children who marry lose eligibility.
- Former spouses lose entitlement effective the date of divorce.
- Remarriage of a surviving spouse ends entitlement unless remarriage occurs on or after the 55th birthday or if remarriage before 55 ends, the benefit can be reestablished by reapplying.
- A Veteran's primary caregiver under the Program of Comprehensive Assistance for Family Caregivers (PCAFC) can also be eligible if they do not have other health insurance coverage.

These criteria define who can qualify for CHAMPVA coverage.

**Sources:**
¹ [Eligibility](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#eligibility)  
² [Additional Eligibility](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#additionaleligibilit)

### Question 179: CHAMPVA-4

**Query:** How do I apply for CHAMPVA?

**Category:** CHAMPVA

**Response Time:** 4.35s

**Answer:**

To apply for CHAMPVA, you need to complete the following forms:

- VA Form 10-10d
- VA Form 10-7959C

After completing these forms, you can submit your application either by fax or mail:

- Fax to: 303-331-7809
- Mail to:  
  VHA Office of Community Care  
  CHAMPVA Eligibility  
  PO Box 137  
  Spring City, PA 19475

Make sure to include copies of any supporting or expediting documents you have as well¹.

If you want to file a claim online, you can do so through the VA website link provided in the filing instructions².

### Question 180: CHAMPVA-5

**Query:** Is CHAMPVA the same as TRICARE?

**Category:** CHAMPVA

**Response Time:** 4.11s

**Answer:**

No, CHAMPVA is not the same as TRICARE. 

- CHAMPVA provides health care coverage for the spouse, widow(er), and children of a Veteran who is permanently and totally disabled due to service-connected conditions, or who died due to such conditions or on active duty, provided the dependents are not otherwise eligible for TRICARE benefits.  
- To be eligible for CHAMPVA, the beneficiary cannot be eligible for TRICARE.  
- TRICARE is a separate Department of Defense health care program primarily for active duty service members, retirees, and their families, while CHAMPVA is a VA program for certain survivors and dependents who do not qualify for TRICARE.  

This distinction is emphasized multiple times in the eligibility criteria and FAQs for CHAMPVA¹⁴⁵⁷.

**Sources:**

¹ [Civilian Health and Medical Program of the Department of Veterans Affairs (CHAMPVA) - Additional Eligibility](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#additionaleligibilit)  
⁴ [Frequently Asked Questions about CHAMPVA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#frequently)  
⁵ [Eligibility for CHAMPVA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#eligibility)

**Sources Cited:**
- [References](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#references)

- [CHAMPVA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva)

### Question 182: CHAMPVA-7

**Query:** What is the CHAMPVA deductible?

**Category:** CHAMPVA

**Response Time:** 2.22s

**Answer:**

The CHAMPVA outpatient deductible is $50 per beneficiary per calendar year, with a maximum of $100 per family per calendar year. After the deductible is satisfied, there is a patient cost share of 25% of the allowable amount up to a catastrophic cap of $3,000 per calendar year¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Frequently Asked Questions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#frequently)

### Question 183: CHAMPVA-8

**Query:** Can my spouse get CHAMPVA?

**Category:** CHAMPVA

**Response Time:** 3.55s

**Answer:**

Your spouse can be eligible for CHAMPVA if you meet one of the following conditions:

- You are rated Permanently and Totally disabled due to service-connected condition(s);
- You were rated Permanently and Totally disabled due to service-connected condition(s) at the time of death;
- You died due to a service-connected condition; or
- You died on Active Duty and your dependents are NOT otherwise eligible for Department of Defense TRICARE benefits.

Additionally, your spouse cannot be eligible for TRICARE to qualify for CHAMPVA. If your spouse remarries, their CHAMPVA entitlement generally ends unless the remarriage occurs on or after their 55th birthday or if a remarriage before age 55 ends later (in which case they can reapply)¹.

**Sources:**

### Question 186: CHAMPVA-11

**Query:** Can children use CHAMPVA?

**Category:** CHAMPVA

**Response Time:** 4.15s

**Answer:**

Yes, children can use CHAMPVA if they are dependents of a Veteran who meets the eligibility criteria. Specifically, CHAMPVA coverage is available to the spouse or widow(er) and to the children who are:

- Aged 0-17 years,
- Aged 18-23 years if enrolled in school,
- Aged 18 or older if classified as a helpless child.

Additionally, children who marry will lose eligibility for CHAMPVA coverage¹.

### Question 189: CHAMPVA-14

**Query:** Can I use CHAMPVA with Medicare?

**Category:** CHAMPVA

**Response Time:** 2.7s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can use CHAMPVA with Medicare. CHAMPVA generally pays the lesser of either 75% of the allowable amount after the $50 calendar year deductible is met or the remainder of the charges after Medicare has paid. This means CHAMPVA acts as a secondary payer to Medicare, covering costs that Medicare does not cover, subject to CHAMPVA's deductible and cost-share rules¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Frequently Asked Questions](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#frequently)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Civilian Health and Medical Program of the Department of Veterans Affairs (CHAMPVA)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/survivorbenefits#top)

- [Additional Eligibility](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#additionaleligibilit)
- [Benefits](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#benefits)
- [Eligibility](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#eligibility)
- [Frequently (Part 3)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#frequently)
- [Frequently (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#frequently)

### Question 190: CHAMPVA-15

**Query:** What happens to CHAMPVA if I remarry?

**Category:** CHAMPVA

**Response Time:** 3.04s

**Answer:**

If you remarry, your CHAMPVA entitlement will end unless:

- Your remarriage takes place on or after your 55th birthday; or
- You remarry before age 55, and that remarriage ends (in which case you would need to reapply to reestablish the benefit).

This applies specifically to dependents who are spouses or widow(er)s of Veterans eligible for CHAMPVA¹.

**Sources:**

### Question 191: 1151-1

**Query:** What is a 1151 claim?

**Category:** 1151

**Response Time:** 7.78s

**Answer:**

A 1151 claim is a type of claim for compensation for disabilities caused, disabilities made worse, or death as a result of VA care or a compensated work therapy program. Specifically, it covers:

- Aggravation of pre-existing conditions
- New disabilities
- Deaths

These must be caused by VA carelessness, negligence, lack of proper skill, or error in judgment during VA medical treatment or related programs¹³.

To win a 1151 claim, the Veteran must show:

1. An event related to VA care that meets specific criteria.
2. Disability or death that began or worsened after this event.
3. A medical nexus opinion linking the event to the disability or death¹.

Additionally, 1151 claims can sometimes be filed for care done outside a VA Medical Center if the VA referred the Veteran and the resulting disability or death was due to VA negligence³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Winning a 1151 Claim - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#winninga1151claim)  
³ [Frequently - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#frequently)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Winning a 1151 Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#winninga1151claim)
- [1151 Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151)
- [Frequently](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#frequently)

### Question 192: 1151-2

**Query:** What is a federal tort claim?

**Category:** 1151

**Response Time:** 4.56s

**Answer:**

A federal tort claim is a claim a Veteran can file when they are injured by a federal government employee who was acting within the scope of their official duties, and the injury was caused by the employee's negligent or wrongful act. To win such a claim, the Veteran must prove:

- The injury was caused by a federal government employee;
- The employee was acting within the scope of their official duties;
- The employee acted negligently or wrongfully;
- The negligent or wrongful act directly caused the injury complained of.

The claim must be submitted using Standard Form 95 to the Department of Veterans Affairs Office of General Counsel, Torts Law Group. It is highly recommended to seek an attorney specialized in federal tort claims. Additionally, the claim must be received within 2 years of the injury, and unlike an 1151 claim, the federal government has no duty to assist in gathering evidence for the claim, so the claimant must provide all necessary evidence themselves¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Winning a Federal Tort Claim - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#winningafederaltortc)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Winning a Federal Tort Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#winningafederaltortc)
- [1151 Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151)

**Confidence:** 0.6689102649688721

### Question 193: 1151-3

**Query:** Can I sue the VA for malpractice?

**Category:** 1151

**Response Time:** 5.77s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can sue the VA for malpractice through a Federal Tort Claim. To win such a claim, you must show:

- You were injured by a federal government employee;
- The employee was acting within the scope of their official duties;
- The employee acted negligently or wrongfully;
- The negligent or wrongful act directly caused your injury.

You or your legal representative need to complete Standard Form 95 and submit it to the VA Office of General Counsel, Torts Law Group. It is highly recommended to seek an attorney who specializes in federal tort claims. Note that the claim must be received within 2 years of the injury, and unlike VA disability claims, the federal government has no Duty to Assist in gathering evidence for a tort claim, so you must provide all supporting evidence yourself¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Winning a Federal Tort Claim - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#winningafederaltortc)

### Question 194: 1151-4

**Query:** How do I file a 1151 claim?

**Category:** 1151

**Response Time:** 9.23s

**Answer:**

To file a 1151 claim, you do not need a special or separate form. You simply need to identify your claim as a 1151 claim when filing your VA disability claim. The claim must include the following elements:

- An event that meets the 1151 criteria (such as an injury or disability caused by VA medical care).
- A disability or death that began or worsened after the event.
- A Nexus letter or independent medical opinion linking the event to the disability or death.

When filing, you should provide details about where and approximately when the event occurred, and explain how the VA provider was at fault.

You can file your claim in several ways:

- Using a Veterans Service Officer (VSO).
- Online at VA.gov.
- By completing and submitting VA Form 21-526EZ online, by fax, mail, or in person at a VA Regional Office.

Make sure to clearly state that your claim is a 1151 claim to assist the VA in processing it properly¹³.

**Sources:**
¹ [Winning a 1151 Claim - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#winninga1151claim)  
³ [Filing a Claim - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 198: 1151-8

**Query:** Can I get compensation for VA medical error?

**Category:** 1151

**Response Time:** 6.72s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get compensation for VA medical error under a 1151 claim if certain conditions are met. Compensation can be paid for disabilities that result from VA medical care errors, including:

- Acts of omission such as failure to obtain informed consent prior to treatment or failure to provide timely diagnosis or treatment.
- Errors involving negligence or error in judgment by VA medical personnel.
- Disability or death caused by direct participation in a compensated work therapy program.
- Failure to diagnose or treat a condition when a physician exercising proper skill and care would have reasonably been able to do so, and this failure resulted in disability¹.

Additionally, the disability or death must be caused by VA carelessness, negligence, lack of proper skill, or error in judgment. If the care was provided outside a VA Medical Center but was referred by the VA, and the VA's negligence caused the disability or death, you may also file a 1151 claim².

Therefore, if you suffered a disability or death as a result of VA medical error that meets these criteria, you may be eligible for compensation through a 1151 claim.

**Sources:**
¹ [Detailed Explanation of Criteria (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#detailedexplanationo)  
² [Frequently - 1151 Claims](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/1151#frequently)

**Sources Cited:**

### Question 200: 1151-10

**Response Time:** 4.43s

**Answer:**

### Question 202: AO-2

**Query:** What conditions are presumptive for Agent Orange?

**Category:** AO

**Response Time:** 6.82s

**Answer:**

The presumptive diseases for Agent Orange exposure include:

- Chloracne or other acneform disease consistent with chloracne
- Early-onset peripheral neuropathy
- Porphyria cutanea tarda

These conditions must have developed within one year of the last possible exposure and reached at least a 10% severity within that year to be considered presumptive¹.

Additional presumptive conditions include ischemic heart disease (excluding peripheral vascular disease or stroke), and various types of soft-tissue sarcomas such as adult fibrosarcoma, alveolar soft part sarcoma, angiosarcoma, clear cell sarcoma of tendons and aponeuroses, dermatofibrosarcoma protuberans, epithelioid leiomyosarcoma, leiomyosarcoma, liposarcoma, malignant fibrous histiocytoma, malignant schwannoma, rhabdomyosarcoma, synovial sarcoma, among others. 

Some conditions like bladder cancer and hypertension do not have to manifest to at least a 10% evaluation to be presumptive².

**Sources:**
¹ [Presumptive Diseases - (presumptive)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#presumptivediseases)  
² [Presumptive Diseases Continued (Part 2) - (presumptive)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#presumptivediseasesc)

### Question 203: AO-3

**Query:** What is the Nehmer rule?

**Category:** AO

**Response Time:** 9.93s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **48.6%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

The Nehmer rule refers to a VA policy that applies when a new presumptive condition related to Agent Orange exposure is added to the list of conditions recognized by the VA under 38 CFR 3.309(e). Under this rule, the VA is required to review prior denied claims and grant earlier effective dates for service connection if there was evidence of the condition before it became presumptive. This means that veterans who had a diagnosis of the condition before it was officially recognized as presumptive can receive service connection effective from the date of their earlier claim or diagnosis, even if their original claim was denied before the condition became presumptive.

Key points about the Nehmer rule include:

- It applies only to conditions named in 38 CFR 3.309(e), excluding chloracne.
- It does not apply to claims filed after the condition became presumptive, but those claims may still be eligible under liberalizing laws.
- Some conditions like hypertension and MGUS are currently not eligible for Nehmer consideration.
- The VA must grant earlier effective dates as appropriate without the veteran needing to file a new claim for the earlier date.

For example, if a veteran filed a claim for lung cancer before it became a presumptive condition and had medical evidence of the diagnosis at that time, the VA must grant service connection effective the date of the initial claim, even if the claim was denied initially.

**Sources:**
¹ [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 3) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)  
² [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 4) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 4)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)
- [Nehmer Class Members (38 CFR 3.816) (Part 3)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#nehmerclassmembers38)

**Confidence:** 0.485615611076355

### Question 204: AO-4

**Query:** Am I eligible for Agent Orange benefits?

**Category:** AO

**Response Time:** 11.0s

**Answer:**

Your eligibility for Agent Orange benefits depends primarily on where and when you served, as well as certain special duties. Specifically, you may be eligible if you served in any of the following locations during the specified dates:

- American Samoa (including territorial waters): January 9, 1962 – July 30, 1980
- Cambodia (Mimot or Krek, Kampong Cham Province): April 16, 1969 – April 30, 1969
- Guam (including territorial waters): January 9, 1962 – July 30, 1980
- Johnston Atoll or on a ship that called at Johnston Atoll: January 1, 1972 – September 30, 1977
- Korean DMZ (in or near): September 1, 1967 – August 31, 1971
- Laos: December 1, 1965 – September 30, 1969
- Republic of Vietnam (including Brown Water Navy and Blue Water Navy service): January 9, 1962 – May 7, 1975
- Thailand (any U.S. or Royal Thai military base): January 9, 1962 – June 30, 1976

Additionally, if you served on active duty or as a reservist in an Air Force unit where a C-123 aircraft with traces of Agent Orange was assigned and had repeated contact with this aircraft due to your duties, you may also be eligible.

There are also non-presumptive locations where Agent Orange was stored, destroyed, or tested. Service in these locations does not automatically grant presumptive exposure, but if you can provide evidence that exposure was at least as likely as not, the VA can concede exposure³.

If you meet these service criteria and have developed a related condition, you may be eligible for Agent Orange benefits.

**Sources:**
¹ [Eligibility (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)  
² [Agent Orange - (overview)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange)  
³ [Eligibility (Part 2)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Agent Orange](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange)
- [Eligibility (Part 1)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)
- [Eligibility (Part 2)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)

### Question 206: AO-6

**Query:** Is diabetes presumptive for Agent Orange?

**Category:** AO

**Response Time:** 3.03s

**Answer:**

Yes, diabetes mellitus is considered a presumptive condition related to Agent Orange exposure. The VA recognizes diabetes mellitus (including Type 1 and Type 2 diabetes) as a condition that can be presumed to be caused by Agent Orange exposure, meaning veterans who were exposed to Agent Orange and develop diabetes may be eligible for VA disability benefits without needing to prove direct service connection¹.

### Question 207: AO-7

**Query:** Is Parkinson's connected to Agent Orange?

**Category:** AO

**Response Time:** 3.02s

**Answer:**

Yes, Parkinson's Disease is connected to Agent Orange exposure. It is listed as a presumptive condition for veterans who were exposed to Agent Orange, meaning that if a veteran has Parkinson's Disease and was exposed to Agent Orange, the VA will presume the disease is related to that exposure for disability compensation purposes.

### Question 209: AO-9

**Query:** Can my children get benefits from Agent Orange?

**Category:** AO

**Response Time:** 4.35s

**Answer:**

Yes, your children may be eligible for benefits related to Agent Orange exposure through the Chapter 35 Dependents Educational Assistance (DEA) program. This is an auxiliary benefit available to dependents of veterans who have qualifying Agent Orange-related conditions¹.

Additionally, there are historical eligibility requirements for dependent children to receive DEA benefits, which include:

- The event that made the child eligible for DEA benefits happened to the Veteran or servicemember before August 1, 2023;
- The child turned 18 before August 1, 2023; and
- The child completed high school before August 1, 2023.

Children must meet all these criteria to qualify under the historical rules, but there may be other current eligibility rules not detailed here².

### Question 210: AO-10

**Query:** Where was Agent Orange used?

**Category:** AO

**Response Time:** 8.33s

**Answer:**

Agent Orange was used in several locations during specific time periods, primarily during the Vietnam War era. These locations include:

- Republic of Vietnam (including Brown Water Navy Service and Blue Water Navy Service within 12 nautical miles of Vietnam, Cambodia) from January 9, 1962, through May 7, 1975
- American Samoa (including territorial waters) from January 9, 1962, through July 30, 1980
- Guam (including territorial waters) from January 9, 1962, through July 30, 1980
- Cambodia (at Mimot or Krek, Kampong Cham Province) from April 16, 1969, through April 30, 1969
- Johnston Atoll or ships that called there from January 1, 1972, through September 30, 1977
- Korean Demilitarized Zone (in or near) from September 1, 1967, through August 31, 1971
- Laos from December 1, 1965, through September 30, 1969
- Thailand (any U.S. or Royal Thai military base) from January 9, 1962, through June 30, 1976

Additionally, Agent Orange was stored, destroyed, or tested at various U.S. and overseas locations, though service at these locations does not automatically mean presumptive exposure unless evidence shows exposure was at least as likely as not¹³².

**Sources:**
¹ [Eligibility (Part 1) - (eligibility)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)  
² [Eligibility (Part 2) - (eligibility)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/agentorange#eligibility)

### Question 212: DEPENDENTS-2

**Query:** What is DIC (Dependency and Indemnity Compensation)?

**Category:** DEPENDENTS

**Response Time:** 7.78s

**Answer:**

Dependency and Indemnity Compensation (DIC) is a tax-free monetary benefit paid to surviving spouses, children, or parents of a Servicemember who died in the line of duty or the survivor of a Veteran who:

- Died from a service-connected illness or injury, including unclaimed complications or aggravations of a service-connected condition;

- Died from something related to service but never formally granted service-connection during their lifetime;

- Died due to VA medical care or treatment;

- Died due to participation in a VA work program such as Veteran Readiness and Employment (VR&E);

- Or did not die from a service-connected illness or injury but was rated as 100% disabled due to service-connected disabilities for a certain period before death (such as 10 years before death, or 5 years immediately before death since release from active duty, or 1 year if a former prisoner of war who died after September 30, 1999)¹.

This benefit is intended to provide financial support to eligible survivors of Veterans or Servicemembers who meet these criteria.

**Sources:**
¹ [Dependency and Indemnity Compensation (DIC)](https://www.va.gov/disability/dependency-indemnity-compensation/)

### Question 213: DEPENDENTS-3

**Query:** How do I add my spouse to my benefits?

**Category:** DEPENDENTS

**Response Time:** 5.77s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **46.4%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

To add your spouse to your VA benefits, you generally need to include them as a dependent when applying or updating your benefits information with the VA. Here are some key points based on the context:

- You must be legally married to the spouse, and the marriage must be active at the time of any relevant claims or benefits¹.
- Spouses remain eligible as dependents so long as you remain married².
- When applying for certain benefits (such as education benefits), the dependent (spouse) must apply from their own account, but spouses still count as dependents for benefits purposes³.
- It is important to notify the VA and provide documentation of your marriage to have your spouse recognized as a dependent.

For specific applications like education benefits, you can apply online and include your spouse as a dependent during that process³.

If you want to ensure your spouse is added to your VA benefits, you should contact the VA directly or use their online portals to update your dependent information.

### Question 214: DEPENDENTS-4

**Query:** What benefits do surviving spouses get?

**Category:** DEPENDENTS

**Response Time:** 9.85s

**Answer:**

Surviving spouses of Veterans are eligible for a variety of benefits, including:

- National Cemetery Burial benefits, allowing interment in a national cemetery even if the Veteran is not buried there¹⁴⁵.
- Civilian Health and Medical Program of the Department of Veterans Affairs (CHAMPVA) healthcare coverage¹⁵.
- Continuance of Claim/Appeal benefits, allowing monetary claims to continue¹⁵.
- Dependency Indemnity Compensation (DIC), a monetary benefit for surviving spouses who meet specific marriage and service criteria¹⁷⁵.
- Dependents Educational Assistance (DEA) for education benefits¹⁵⁶.
- Free Tuition and Educational and Career Counseling (Chapter 36)¹⁵.
- Federal Civil Service Preference for employment¹⁵.
- Life Insurance benefits¹⁵.
- Medal of Honor Pension for eligible surviving spouses¹⁵.
- VA automobile allowance and adaptive equipment, Specially Adapted Housing grants, and potentially Special Monthly Compensation depending on disease progression¹⁶.

Additional eligibility notes include:

- The surviving spouse must not remarry to retain certain benefits like DIC, except under specific age and remarriage date conditions¹⁷.
- Income and net worth limits apply for pension eligibility, with increased rates for spouses who are housebound or require aid and attendance¹.
- Surviving spouses remain eligible for benefits as long as they have not remarried or any remarriage was annulled³.

These benefits provide financial, educational, healthcare, and burial support to surviving spouses of Veterans.

### Question 215: DEPENDENTS-5

**Query:** What is Chapter 35 DEA?

**Category:** DEPENDENTS

**Response Time:** 8.85s

**Answer:**

Chapter 35 DEA (Dependents' Educational Assistance) is a program that provides education benefits to the spouse and dependent children (including step and adopted children) of a Veteran or Servicemember who meets certain criteria. These criteria include:

- The Veteran is permanently and totally disabled from service-connected conditions;
- The Veteran died in service;
- The Veteran died of service-connected causes; or
- The Veteran is currently missing in action or captured in the line of duty¹.

The benefit can be used for pursuing undergraduate or graduate degrees, courses leading to certificates or diplomas from business, technical, or vocational schools, apprenticeship, and on-the-job training programs. Correspondence courses are available to spouses only. The program provides up to 45 months of education or training for beneficiaries who used their Chapter 35 benefits before August 1, 2018, and 36 months for those who use the benefits on or after that date. Each dependent receives their own individual entitlement, and the benefit is paid directly to the dependent².

Additional notes include that children cannot receive both DIC (Dependency and Indemnity Compensation) and DEA at the same time, and the benefit cannot be used for flight school but can be used for overseas programs².

Eligibility requirements vary depending on when the event making the dependent eligible occurred, with different rules for events before or after August 1, 2023, and specific conditions for spouses and children³⁴⁶.

**Sources:**
¹ [Chapter 35 (Dependents' Educational Assistance - DEA) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/pnt)

⁶ [Eligibility Requirements for Dependent Spouses (Historical) - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/time)

### Question 216: DEPENDENTS-6

**Query:** Can my children get VA benefits?

**Category:** DEPENDENTS

**Response Time:** 14.67s

**Answer:**

Your children may be eligible for VA benefits under certain conditions:

- For CHAMPVA coverage, children of a Veteran can be covered if the Veteran is permanently and totally disabled due to service-connected conditions, was rated so at the time of death, died due to a service-connected condition, or died on active duty with dependents not eligible for TRICARE. Children must be aged 0-17, 18-23 if enrolled in school, or 18+ if a helpless child due to disability before age 18. Children who marry will lose eligibility for CHAMPVA¹.

- For Dependents Educational Assistance (DEA) benefits, children must be dependents of a Veteran and eligible for any VA education benefit³. Eligibility depends on when the event making the child eligible occurred and the child's age and schooling status:
  - If the event happened before August 1, 2023, and the child turned 18 and completed high school before that date, they must be between 18 and 26 years old to get DEA benefits. Marriage does not end eligibility in this case².
  - If the event happened on or after August 1, 2023, or the child turned 18 or completed high school on or after that date, the benefit does not expire. However, children attending approved high schools can use DEA benefits for up to 5 months until August 1, 2026²⁶.

- Children may also be eligible for Dependency and Indemnity Compensation (DIC) as surviving children if they are unmarried, not included on the surviving spouse's compensation, and are under 18, under 23 if attending school, or permanently incapable of self-support due to disability before age 18. If a child marries, they generally lose DIC entitlement unless the marriage ends and was after October 31, 1990⁷.

- To apply for education benefits, the dependent child must apply from their own account and obtain a Certificate of Eligibility to present to their school's Certifying Official⁴.

In summary, your children can get VA benefits if they meet the specific eligibility criteria related to age, schooling, marital status, and the Veteran's service-connected disability or death status.

**Sources:**
¹ [Eligibility - CHAMPVA](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/champva#eligibility)  
² [Eligibility Requirements for Dependent Children (Historical)](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)  
³ [Eligibility - Survivor Benefits](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/survivorbenefits#top)  
⁴ [Applying - Survivor Benefits](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/survivorbenefits#top)  
⁶ [Eligibility Requirements for Dependent Children and Spouses](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

### Question 218: DEPENDENTS-8

**Query:** How do I apply for DIC?

**Category:** DEPENDENTS

**Response Time:** 9.32s

**Answer:**

To apply for Dependency and Indemnity Compensation (DIC), follow these steps based on your relationship to the Veteran or Servicemember:

- If you are the surviving spouse or child of a Servicemember who died while on active duty, your military casualty assistance officer will help you complete an Application for DIC, Death Pension, and/or Accrued Benefits by a Surviving Spouse or Child (VA Form 21P-534a). The officer will assist you in mailing the form to the correct VA regional office.

- If you are the surviving spouse or child of a Veteran, fill out an Application for DIC, Death Pension, and/or Accrued Benefits (VA Form 21P-534EZ). Mail the completed form to the Pension Management Center (PMC) for your state.

- If you are a surviving parent, fill out an Application for Dependency and Indemnity Compensation by Parent(s) (VA Form 21P-535) and mail it to the Pension Management Center (PMC) for your state.

- You can also upload the VA Form 21-534EZ online through the VA's secure portal.

These forms and mailing instructions are available on the VA website and through the links provided in the sources below¹⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [Applying - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/survivorbenefits#top)  
⁴ [Applying - (policy)](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)

### Question 219: DEPENDENTS-9

**Query:** What is survivor pension?

**Category:** DEPENDENTS

**Response Time:** 4.29s

**Answer:**

A survivor pension is a monthly payment provided by the VA to qualified surviving spouses and unmarried dependent children of wartime Veterans. To be eligible, the surviving spouse or dependent must meet certain income and net worth limits set by Congress. The pension payments will be stopped if the recipient is incarcerated for over 60 days due to a felony or misdemeanor conviction or is a fugitive felon. To apply for apportionment of the pension, VA Form 21-0788 must be completed and submitted to the VA by the surviving spouse or their dependents¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Survivors Pension](https://www.va.gov/pension/survivors-pension/)

### Question 220: DEPENDENTS-10

**Query:** Can I get benefits if my veteran parent died?

**Category:** DEPENDENTS

**Response Time:** 9.65s

**Answer:**

Yes, you may be eligible for benefits if your veteran parent died, but it depends on your relationship and status as a surviving dependent.

- If you are a surviving child, you may be eligible for various survivor benefits such as Dependency Indemnity Compensation (DIC), Life Insurance, and educational benefits¹.
- If you are a surviving parent of a deceased veteran, you may also be eligible for benefits such as Dependency Indemnity Compensation (DIC), Life Insurance, and Federal Civil Service Preference¹.
- To be eligible as a surviving parent, you must be unmarried, or married to someone who is permanently and totally disabled (not necessarily VA determined), or legally separated from your spouse³.
- Additionally, to receive compensation as a surviving parent, your income must be below a certain amount, and you must be the biological, adoptive, or foster parent of the veteran or servicemember⁷.
- Foster parents qualify if they served in the role of a parent before the veteran’s last entry into active service⁷.
- To apply for benefits as a surviving parent, you need to fill out VA Form 21P-535 and mail it to the Pension Management Center for your state⁵.

Therefore, if you meet these criteria, you can apply for survivor benefits related to your deceased veteran parent.

### Question 221: EDGE-1

**Query:** Can I get VA benefits with a bad conduct discharge?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 8.32s

**Answer:**

Generally, to be eligible for VA disability benefits, your character of discharge must be Honorable or General (Under Honorable Conditions)¹. A Bad Conduct Discharge (BCD) is typically considered a punitive discharge and is not the same as Honorable or General discharge.

However, there are some nuances:

- Even if your character of discharge was not Honorable, you may still be eligible in some cases¹.
- If you have an Other than Honorable discharge and a mental health diagnosis, you should apply to have your character of discharge upgraded, which could improve eligibility for benefits⁶.
- Veterans convicted of certain crimes or with a dishonorable discharge are generally ineligible for benefits².
- Conditions resulting from wilful misconduct or during a period of dishonorable conduct are not service-connected and thus not eligible for benefits³.

Therefore, if you have a Bad Conduct Discharge, you are generally not eligible for VA benefits unless you successfully apply for and receive a discharge upgrade or meet other specific exceptions.

**Sources:**
¹ [Eligibility - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/upgrade)  
² [Eligibility - (notes)](https://veteransbenefitskb.com)  
³ [Wilful Misconduct or Dishonorable Conduct - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/norate)  
⁶ [Discharge Upgrade - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

- [Eligibility](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/upgrade)

**Category:** EDGE

### Question 223: EDGE-3

**Query:** Can I get benefits with an other than honorable discharge?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 6.09s

**Answer:**

Generally, to be eligible for VA disability benefits, your character of discharge must be Honorable or General (Under Honorable)⁵⁷. However, if you have an Other than Honorable (OTH) discharge and a mental health diagnosis, you should apply to have your character of discharge updated, which may improve your eligibility for benefits¹.

There is also a note that even if your character of discharge was not Honorable, you may still be eligible in some cases², but the general rule requires an Honorable or General (Under Honorable) discharge.

In summary:

- Benefits typically require an Honorable or General (Under Honorable) discharge⁵⁷.
- Veterans with an OTH discharge and a mental health diagnosis should apply for a discharge upgrade¹.
- Some exceptions may exist, but eligibility with an OTH discharge is not guaranteed².

### Question 224: EDGE-4

**Query:** What if I was dishonorably discharged?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 5.15s

**Answer:**

If you were dishonorably discharged, the VA will **NOT** grant service connection for conditions that are a direct result of wilful misconduct or occurred during a period of dishonorable conduct. Examples of wilful misconduct include being in AWOL or deserter status, drunk driving, or intentionally injuring yourself in a fight or duel. Additionally, eligibility for VA benefits generally requires that the veteran's character of discharge was **NOT** dishonorable⁴.

However, if you have an Other than Honorable discharge and a mental health diagnosis, you may apply to have your character of discharge updated, which could improve your eligibility for benefits¹.

### Question 225: EDGE-5

**Query:** Can reservists get VA disability?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 8.78s

**Answer:**

Yes, reservists can get VA disability compensation if they incur or aggravate an injury, illness, or disease while in the line of duty. This includes periods of Active Duty for Training (ADT), Inactive Duty Training (IADT), drill weekends, or any other training while on orders, as well as traveling directly to or from their place of duty. To properly file a disability claim, reservists should have their unit issue a Line of Duty (LOD) determination or a Notice of Eligibility (NOE) if in the U.S. Coast Guard Reserve. If a LOD/NOE is not obtained, they may rely on buddy letters or personal statements as evidence. Additionally, having pre- and post-deployment physicals can support the claim if accurate and complete.

Furthermore, eligibility for certain benefits related to reservists includes having had Servicemembers' Group Life Insurance (SGLI) while on duty and suffering an injury or disability that disqualified them for standard premium insurance rates, or being within specific time frames related to release from active duty or reserve status³⁴.

**Sources:**
¹ [National Guard/Reserves - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim#top)  
² [Eligibility - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/upgrade)  
³ [Eligibility - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)  
⁴ [Eligibility - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/ears)

### Question 226: EDGE-6

**Query:** Can National Guard get VA benefits?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 8.8s

**Answer:**

Yes, National Guard members can get VA benefits if they incur or aggravate an injury, illness, or disease while in the line of duty, such as during Active Duty for Training (ADT), Inactive Duty Training (IADT), drill weekends, or any other training while on orders. This coverage also includes traveling directly to or from their place of duty. To properly file a disability claim, they should have their unit issue a Line of Duty (LOD) determination or a Notice of Eligibility (NOE) if in the U.S. Coast Guard Reserve. If they do not obtain an LOD/NOE, they will need to rely on buddy letters or personal statements as evidence. Pre- and post-deployment physicals can also serve as evidence if accurate and complete¹.

Additionally, eligibility for VA disability benefits requires that the disability is chronic and related to military service, the character of discharge is honorable (or possibly general under honorable), and the service included Active Duty, ADT, or IADT, which applies to National Guard members as well³.

Furthermore, eligibility conditions include being within certain time frames related to release or retirement from the National Guard or Ready Reserves².

### Question 227: EDGE-7

**Query:** What is character of discharge?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.21s

**Answer:**

The "character of discharge" refers to the classification of a veteran's separation from military service, which can affect their eligibility for VA benefits. It is a designation that indicates the nature of the discharge, such as Honorable, Other than Honorable, or other types. This classification is important because certain types of discharges, like an Other than Honorable discharge, may limit access to VA benefits unless upgraded. Veterans with mental health diagnoses who have an Other than Honorable discharge are encouraged to apply for a discharge upgrade to potentially improve their eligibility for benefits.

### Question 228: EDGE-8

**Query:** Can I get VA benefits if I never deployed?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.59s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can get VA benefits even if you never deployed. The basic eligibility requirements for VA disability benefits include having a chronic disability that was the result of your military service, a preexisting condition made worse by your military service, or a disability that resulted from an already service-connected disability. Deployment is not listed as a requirement. Additionally, you must have an Honorable or General Under Honorable discharge and have served on Active Duty, Active Duty for training, or Inactive Duty Training¹.

### Question 229: EDGE-9

**Query:** What if my condition started after service?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 7.02s

**Answer:**

If your condition started after service, it may still be possible to get it rated if:

- The condition is a congenital or genetic condition that manifested during your service after the time period medical science has determined it should have developed¹.

- The condition got worse due to your service or a service-connected condition (aggravated by service)¹.

- The condition is a presumptive secondary condition linked to a service-connected condition such as traumatic brain injury (TBI), where certain conditions diagnosed after service are presumed to be related to the in-service injury².

- You can establish a nexus (connection) between the in-service event or service-connected disability and the current condition, supported by medical evidence or lay statements⁶.

In general, for a condition that started after service, you need to provide evidence of a connection to your service or a service-connected disability to have a chance at service-connection.

**Sources:**
¹ [Congenital or Genetic Conditions - (policy)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/norate)  
² [Presumptive Secondary Conditions - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/claimtype#secondary)

### Question 230: EDGE-10

**Query:** Can I get benefits for conditions that existed before service?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 3.27s

**Answer:**

You can get VA benefits for conditions that existed before service only under certain circumstances:

- If the condition manifested during your service after the time period medical science has determined it should have developed, it can be rated.

- If the condition got worse due to your service or a service-connected condition (i.e., it was aggravated), it can be rated.

- For Active Duty service members who serve for at least 8 years, there are additional considerations related to DoD Medboards.

Otherwise, congenital or genetic conditions (those you were born with) generally are not rated unless these exceptions apply¹.

### Question 234: EDGE-14

**Query:** Can I file a claim without medical records?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 8.35s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **49.9%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

You can file a claim without submitting your own medical records, but it is important to provide sufficient evidence to support your claim. The VA can obtain your VA medical records themselves, so it is not necessary to upload them as part of your claim. However, if you have private medical records or non-VA hospital reports related to your claimed condition, you should provide them or give the VA a VA Form 21-4142/21-4142a to obtain those records for you. 

Additionally, before filing a claim, you must have at least these three parts:

- A statement of symptoms or current diagnosis.
- An in-service event or service-connected disability that caused or worsened your condition.
- A nexus (a layman would think the first two are related).

If your claim does not have all three parts, it will be denied without even being sent to a Compensation & Pension Exam (C&P)¹⁴⁵.

So while medical records are not strictly required to file a claim, you do need sufficient evidence and statements to support your claim, and providing medical records can help establish your case.

**Sources:**

¹ [Miscellaneous Evidence - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)  
² [BARE MINIMUM You Need BEFORE You File a Claim: - (references)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)

### Question 236: EDGE-16

**Query:** Can I get benefits for conditions not in my service record?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 6.64s

**Answer:**

You can potentially get benefits for conditions not explicitly documented in your service record, but certain criteria must be met:

- You need to provide a statement of symptoms or a current diagnosis.
- You must show an in-service event or a service-connected disability that caused or worsened your condition.
- There must be a nexus, meaning a layperson would think the symptoms or diagnosis and the in-service event or service-connected disability are related.

If your claim does not have all three parts, it will likely be denied without even being sent for a Compensation & Pension Exam (C&P)¹.

Additionally, if you have medical records outside your military service treatment records (STRs), you must coordinate to have those records added to your military records before separation, as private medical records are generally not included by default².

Therefore, while conditions not in your service record can be considered, you must provide sufficient evidence linking the condition to your service or a service-connected disability.

**Sources:**
¹ [BARE MINIMUM You Need BEFORE You File a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)  
² [Active Duty Servicemembers](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

### Question 238: EDGE-18

**Query:** Can family members provide statements?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.52s

**Answer:**

⚠️ **Note:** My sources may not fully cover this topic. Our internal confidence score is **45.3%** for this response - please verify the answer with your own due diligence. A report has been generated and someone will review this.

Yes, family members can provide statements, known as Buddy Statements, to support your claim. These statements can come from family members, friends, clergy members, law enforcement personnel, or those you served with. They can describe your claimed condition, how and when it happened, how it worsened, and how it currently affects you¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Miscellaneous Evidence - (notes)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

**Sources Cited:**
- [Miscellaneous Evidence](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)

### Question 240: EDGE-20

**Query:** Can I request a different C&P examiner?

**Category:** EDGE

**Response Time:** 2.42s

**Answer:**

Yes, you can request a different C&P examiner or specify a particular contractor or VA medical center for your Compensation and Pension exam. You can include a personal statement with your request to indicate your preference. The VA will attempt to honor this request if it is reasonable and possible. Additionally, you can request a tele-health appointment for your mental health exam if you prefer¹.

**Sources:**
¹ [Compensation & Pension Examinations (C&P)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)

**Sources Cited:**
- [[Compensation & Pension Examinations (C&P)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/mental)
- [After Submitting a Claim](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/vaclaim)
- [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)
- [Disability Benefits Questionnaire (DBQs)](https://www.veteransbenefitskb.com/cnp)
